<answer>



They can certainly take more tuition indeed. That's what instructors are there for. Most will think that they just teach Learners to test standard, but that is totally false.

A Driving instructor is there to offer different levels of tuition based on your knowledge and personal ability.

The Pass Plus course incorporates two hours of several aspects, night driving, bad whether, and motorway driving. This can only be taken within 12 months of passing the practical car test.

If I was ever stuck or hadn't driven for a while, I would definately call a driving instructor, they can become very helpful.
GDJ.



</answer>

<question>



Changing tire size with rims?



Right now my tires are 215/60/17.  I am looking at 16" rims with new tires from tirerack.com.  With the bigger rims, obviously I need lower profile tires.  The size tirerack.com recommends with the package is 205/55/16.  Is this correct?  Also, any opinions about tirerack.com would be greatly appreciated.



</question>



<answer from>



PMack
Member since: 27 December 2006
Total points: 13629 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



I think there's something wrong with your data - according to what you wrote, your current wheels are larger that what you are looking at (17 vs 16). Are your current ones 215/60/15? That would make more sense.



If you want the speedometer/odometer to be accurate without have to have it recalibrated, you want to make sure the new tires are approximately the same diameter as the old ones. If the old ones are 215/60/15's, that means they are approximately 25 inches in diameter, as would the 205/55/16's (all of this info is available on tirerack).



Do you really want a narrower tire (the 205 or 215 is the width)? I would go for a 225/50/16 if I were you, it will look better.



As for tirerack - they are excellent, I have bought from them before and will do so again. Read the customer reviews of the tires you are considering, you can get the lesser known brands for a lot less money, and they are often excellent tires. Kumho, Summitomo, Hankook - all good tires for the money.



Good luck!



</answer>



<asker's comment>



Yes, mine are 60R15 (not 17).  Would I be able to go with the 225's, or would that interfere with the suspension/steering/etc.?



</asker's comment>



<comments>



PMack



225 is only 10 mm wider than what you have. Just make sure the new wheels have the same offset as the old ones.



</comments>



<answer from>



kelly_f_...
Member since: 22 September 2006
Total points: 8616 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



what is the tire size on the driver door is the best size and something to go off of.. dont see any problems with that number size



</answer>



<answer from>



PH3
Member since: 17 August 2008
Total points: 113 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



PMack has a very good answer, but I would like to add that if you put a lower profile tire on your car without lowering it, you may not be happy with how it looks.  A good rule of thumb visually is that the gap between the top of the tire and the bottom of your fender should never be larger than the hight of your tire's sidewall.  If you are concidering new rims I would assume that you are doing it for visual reasons.  The question I would be asking if I were you is:  How big of a tire can I fit on my car without rubbing?  Be sure to include vehicle information and any modifications done to the suspension.



Source(s):



I work here... www.purevisiondesign.com



</answer>

<question>



Peugeot 1.9td engine?

<answer from>



fastbob
Member since: 03 August 2007
Total points: 735 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



could you be more specific,,i also have a pug 306 1.9tdi,generally speaking these engines are bombproof,peugot make excellent diesel engines.

Mine ticks,rattles at times,it will do it's a diesel.

When do you hear it?,,while driving or when idling with the bonnet up ??
Is it loud,sharp - when hot or cold ?

Some more information would help.

The thing with diesels are they are simpler than a petrol and although heavier and running 2 - 3 times the compression they will tend to last longer,as long as it's not burning oil and the cam belt is changed when it's specified there is little major to go wrong !!!!

jerry o

<answer>



Had a 1.9td engine for years, they are a great engine. Have had a ticking problem with a similar engine and it was due to a haudraulic lifter (tappet) sticking. It could be a sign that the oil needs changing, but it may need changing. Good news is that I would only worry when it is more constant and the repair is reasonably cheap - I believe the part is under £30, probably cheaper if you shop around.



</answer>

<answer from>



Dave J
Member since: 27 March 2008

Contributing In:



Other - Cars & Transportation

<answer>



terrible and terrible! As with most modern cars the visibility is appauling, especially when trying to reverse - which, let's face it, is a pretty important part of learning to drive!

The previous corsa was a cracking car, with excellent visibility, it was a dream to teach people in - but i went and test drove a new one an knew straight away it would be a bad.

As far as the touch sensitive indicators - absolute nightmare - I don't know if there is a menu in the settings enabling you to turn off the "lane change (3 flashes)" function because it is a nightmare for learners who try to cancel the signal, and end up indicating the other way then panic and indicate the other way! Disco lights on the car!

The interior is bland, the astra is pretty ugly, but the corsa does look quite nice from the outside, but as far as teaching someone, I think they score pretty poorly.



Source(s):

<question from>



frank
Member since: 09 August 2007
Total points: 102 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



What do i look for when i test drive a car?



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



it really depends on where you're buying it and how old it is.  new car?  take it out and see if you like the way it drives.  used car (less than 10 years)  pop the hood and make sure it all looks in order.  check for oil on the engine and rust on parts.  aside from the obvious, the best way to find problems with a used car is to go out in the country and act like you're at a drag race.  floor it to about 80, observe any sounds or vibrations that seem out of place, then stop the car as fast as possible.  that will usually tell you if something major is wrong with it.



</answer>

Rover K series engine?



Hi i have been asked if i could fit a turbo to a 1.4 k series engine on a mg zr and was wondering if they even put at turbo on that engine so i could get one from scrapyard or what other turbos would fit easy. I have been told that it might catch on the bonnet is this true? Thanks



</question>



<additional details>



I know it can be done i just dont want to have to  modify the manifold etc as it would be easyer if i could find one that fits on. I no about the head gasket and it aint a problem

<answer from>



J G
Member since: 19 May 2008
Total points: 14231 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Maintenance & Repairs



Other - Cars & Transportation



</answer from>



<answer>



You can't - the compression would need to be lowered, and with an engine so prone to head gasket failure, you'd be asking for trouble.



</answer>



<answer from>



desert camel
Member since: 01 June 2008
Total points: 14544 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Maintenance & Repairs



Motorcycles



</answer from>



<answer>



JG is correct,there a very soft engine....there isnt a turbo manifold that will bolt strait on,it will have to be modded to fit...a spacer plate will lowwer compresion for the turbo boost...anything is possable,i fitted a turbo from a vauxhall 17td to a yamaha xt600....

<question from>



peter d
Member since: 11 January 2007
Total points: 128 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Road haulage pricing?



i am wondering if there is an easy formula to follow when when customers ask for quotes. i.e what to include, what to leave out, profit margin etc, i am moving up to the office soon due to injury and want a bit of a head start, so if there are any road hauliers out there. can you help. thnx.



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



There is no easy formula.

What each hauler charges will depend on their individual overheads and what each company expects to make on margins.  It will be different for each firm.

The only to know for sure, is to ask someone else who works with you, what calculations they are told to use.



</answer>

<question>



Why do you need no seatbelts in front of bus but you do in back of car?



</question>



<answer from>



roflyoun...
Member since: 31 October 2006
Total points: 609 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



My take on it is because since a bus is such a huge vehicle, a crash wouldn't be fatal. Unless a driver RAMMED into the side of the bus, but it's improbable.



</answer>



<answer from>



Mark B
Member since: 17 August 2008
Total points: 131 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



why dont you walk theres a question



</answer>



<answer from>



gunplumb...
Member since: 28 March 2007
Total points: 13713 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



Politics and liability concerns. If seat belts were provided in a bus but the operator failed to ensure they were all being used properly he could conceivably be held responsible for injuries sustained by passengers who weren't belted in. And who wants to drive a bus AND play nanny to every single passenger on it as well? Easier and more financially expedient not to have to deal with the seat belts at all. In cars on the other hand we've had groups lobbying for decades to ensure we're protected from our own stupidity.



</answer>

<answer from>



no answerer details



</answer from>



<answer>



He took your car or did he just take the key?

If his name isn't on the title or on the registration, you are the car's owner and he's the borrower. In an ideal world, he would be the owner, because he's paying for it. But unless his name is on the registration, he'd have to go to civil court to get it.

Legally, if there was a problem with the car, the police would talk to the person who is registered or to the person who is on the title. The person who is registered is required to keep the tags paid and keep insurance on it. You'd get the ticket if it wasn't up to date. So the car is technically yours.

If he has the car in his possession, you might have to go to civil court to get it back. But if he only has the key, you just have to get the locks changed or re-keyed and you're good to go.

Good luck.



</answer>

<answer from>



COLIN T
Member since: 18 May 2008
Total points: 5852 (Level 5)



Contributing In:



Singles & Dating



</answer from>



<answer>



If you are the UK it is usual to get a notice of Intended prosecution within 14 days (Posted within 14 days!). The reason for this is the owner of the vehicle is not necessarily the driver and it is 'reasonable' to assume that within a period of 14 days you would remember who was driving the vehicle at the time.  However, people seem to have the idea that this is law, that is not so, it is an advisory guideline from the Association of chief Constables.

My son is a self employed mechanic and he has a little Peugeot that he lends to customers while he is fixing their cars, but also any one of  a dozen friends borrow it now and then.  Earlier this year the car was flashed at 78 in a 70mph area. The first he knew about this was 13 weeks later!  The offence happened the day after he moved house (from a tented house). My son had not been on that particluar road for years but could not remember who had the use of the car that day as up to 6 people a day can drive it!  From what could be worked out eventually the police had sent 3 separate notices to his OLD address, despite him notifying the DVLA of his new one! The property had remained empty as the owner was going to modernise it a bit then sell it.  He explained the circumstances and pointed out that in the intervening 13 weeks the car could have had anything up to a hundred or more drivers and he had not the faintest idea who had been driving it.  He was prosecuted, including failing to identify the driver and got a £265 fine and 5 points on his clean licence.  (The day he got that there was a newspaper report of a Newcastle Footballer being prosecuted for same offence and HE got a 60 quid fine and 3 points on his already soiled licence!)



</answer>

I was looking at the short ram intakes and they are allot cheaper but i don't think they can give you as much of a power boost, am i right or just an idiot? Because internet explanations have been shaky at best.



</question>



<answer from>



xxmendoz...
Member since: 09 April 2006
Total points: 181 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



go with whatever the cheapest intake you can find is. in the end, the only difference aem makes is that thier intakes are carb legal, but this should only matter if you live in california. nowadays every intake has been designed after all the brand named product so it does not really matter. as far as to buying a cold air intake, rather than a short ram, you do get colder air thus getting more oxygen atoms into your combustion chamber. with a short ram intake you get the hot air from the engine until you begin to move and cold air enters the engine bay. With the cold air intake you have the chances of hydrolock if you live i a place where it rains, but a bypass valve would solve that problem.



</answer>

<answer>



I had over 80 points on my record before I got my license suspended for a year.



Dont go to court.. always pay your fines as soon as you get them. That way the judge never sees the fines or your record. Only reason mine got taken was because court was mandatory for a wreck less driving charge.. judge saw I had 80 points and promptly suspended my license for a year.



You can have as many points as you like so long as you dont appear in court and your name isnt on the docket. There isnt a flag system set up that can automatically suspend your license, a judge or county clerk must suspend your license and they wont know how many points you have if you never appear on the docket becuase you pay your fines as soon as you get them without going to court.



Source(s):



This is assuming you are from the U.S and not the U.K. The U.K has some messed up laws.

<answer from>



itsjustm...
Member since: 01 January 2008
Total points: 12747 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



Obey all traffic laws!  Be sure to use turn signals properly.  Not only when turning, but also when pulling away from the curb.  Do Not Talk To The Examiner while you are driving, unless they ask you a question.  They are only there to give you directions and to monitor and grade your driving skills, not for chit-chat.  Chit-chat by beginning drivers is dangerous.  It will take your concentration off the road.  Remember to come to a complete stop at stop signs.  Make sure that you yeild the right of way, in all cases.  Be certain that you do not interfere with ANY pedestrian, they have the right of way in ALL circumstances.  Be certain to fasten seat belt, adjust the seat and mirrors prior to starting the car for the test.  Follow the examiners directions, unless they instruct you to make a turn too late to safely accomodate the instruction.  Remember that should your driving test take you on a freeway, the right hand lane is for normal trafic and the left hand lane is for passing.  Don't drive on in the left hand lane.  No radio on, no ipod on, absolutely no cell phone on!  Turn it off com pletely!



</answer>



<answer from>



Fellside...
Member since: 11 December 2006
Total points: 3539 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



Do everything right!



</answer>

<answer from>



Vertigo Girl
Member since: 08 July 2007
Total points: 460 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



I passed mine 3 months ago at the 11th attempt!!  They don't fail you on really little things - they fail you if you do something dangerous.  I failed most of mine because of my nerves but this time around - i just wasnt bothered if i passed or failed, i just kept thinking that if i fail i will just put in for it again. On my last test - a van cut me up and i was convinced that i had failed.... but i hadn't!! The examiner said it wasn't my fault, so on your test.... just remember that you haven't failed until the examiner tells you that you have at the end of your test!!  What I'm saying is that don't assume that you have failed if you make a mistake because it will put you off, just carry on driving safely and at the very worst - if you have failed, at least you will have had the experience of completing the whole driving test so you will have more experience next time.  And just keep reminding yourself that the examiner is just somebody's mum/dad/sister/brother.... whatever!! They are just normal people who are doing a job just the same as a window cleaner does his/her job!!  They are not really bothered whether you pass or fail, they go into work, do their job, pick up their wages and... that's it.... I bet half of them don't even like their job.... but they like the money (even though the salary is crap!!!)  They are not devils - just "normal" human beings!!!
One last thing...... always remember to look over your shoulder whenever you move off and every time he/she gives you an instruction - ALWAYS look in your mirror as soon as the instruction is given, do this before you do ANYTHING else, if they say "take your next left", don't indicate then check your mirror - check your mirror first then indicate then check mirror and check again, ignore all these people who say you have to move your head to look in the mirror - you don't, examiners are not stupid, they know when you are looking in your mirror and when you are not!! Plus, they will know you are not looking in your mirror when you don't react to what's going on behind you lol!! Seriously - always expect the unexpected, on one of my tests - I had two police cars behind me with their lights flashing at me (urging me to move over) but guess what?? I wasn't checking my mirrors often enough and so I didn't see them for at least 10 seconds that they were there!!!! Guess what........ he failed me lol!!!  Always always expect the unexpected, last but not least.... good luck!!!



Source(s):



Lots of experience of driving tests!!!



</answer>

<question>



I drove a truck with 8 gears, will it be easy to drive a 10 gear then or harder?



I drove an 8 gear truck, does that mean I should be able to drive a 10 gear truck too?



</question>



<answer from>



george d
Member since: 16 May 2007
Total points: 2530 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Just as easy. The gearbox you used would have been a "Range Change" ( 4 over 4). Depending on the manufacturer, the layout could be different. Scania & Volvo do a 9 speed - 4 over 4 with "crawler" gear,and a 14 speed - 3 over 3 with a "split gear" + 2 "crawler",  ZF a 16 speed - 4 over 4 with split. The hardest part is getting used to the extra gears, you do not need to use all of them. With the newer trucks, a lot of manufacturers are going down the automatic route, some with 2 pedal and some with 3 pedals. I have driven both types, and prefer the conventional 3 pedal layout for slow speed maneuvres as there is normal clutch control, against trying to control with just the throttle. Either way, we are classed as  "Professional Drivers" and as such, employers will expect you to be able to drive anything they give you!! - Even Mercedes with the "EPS" gear box.!!



Source(s):



20 plus years class 1 artic driving



</answer>



<asker's comment>



303



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



chevyss3...
Member since: 13 February 2007
Total points: 1037 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



well its obvioulsy a larger truck, make sure you have the CDL required to operate the truck if it is necessary, and therefore if you know how to drive a truck that size the number of gears should not matter



</answer>



<answer from>



Gemini DJ
Member since: 09 January 2008
Total points: 3499 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



There is no difference whatsoever, besides the fact it's two extra gears.
Large Goods Vehicles and Passenger carrying often have between 8, and 16 gears.

Truck Drivers have to take 5 Tests, ridiculous I know, but that's the goverment for you. Money money money...

1. Category B Theory
2. Category B Practical
3. LGV/PCV Theory
4. Category C Practical (or D)
5. Category C + E Practical

B = Car
D = Coach / Bus
C = Lorry
E = General category for any size of trailer (including articulated)



</answer>

<question>

<answer>

<answer from>



jed slade
Member since: 10 August 2007
Total points: 20114 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



A good cabbie will not need satnav,he will have the knowledge.I never needed one as a tanker driver,you only need an A TO Z of each city when you are on multi van drops or C & D,satnav is brilliant in use at sea along the coast, the marine GPS system is safer than radar in tight sea areas along the coast.The road versions fall down miserably in cities and built up areas as they can only be relied on to give you a location ,not directions.If you are travelling down a dual carriageway and it tells you to turn right,you cannot,it takes no notice of centre reservation barriers and one way systems and traffic free zones.



</answer>

You drive up to a road junction, and the traffic lights are not working. What is the correct procedure to take to get across the road.

fire4511

<answer>



An intersection where the traffic lights are out should be treated as a 4 way stop!

Seeing as this is an unsafe intersection, it you can turn right, and then make a U turn down the road safely, and return to the intersection to turn right again onto the road you started on (on the other side) you will be better off!



Source(s):



27 years in the auto business



</answer>

<question>



Which is cheaper-driving 5 miles or getting the bus?



live in wales
unleaded petrol



</question>



<answer from>



Robbie
Member since: 07 March 2008
Total points: 416 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



As a rough rule of thumb if you work on an average cost of 20pence per mile travelling in a car such as a KA or Daewoo Matiz this should account for the cost of petrol as well as tax, insurance and wear and tear, so at 5 miles this would be about £1.



In this instance driving would be at least as cheap as if not cheaper than taking the bus which I believe would cost around £1 - £1.50 depending on the service, not to mention saving time by being direct and also making for a more comfortable journey.

However if you wre driving a 3 ltr V8 4x4 then no doubt the bus would be the cheaper option.

In summary - look at what car you driving and the engine size



</answer>

<answer from>



Steve
Member since: 25 May 2006
Total points: 287 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



It was probably contrived in one of the oil / auto industry's offices (I think they're in bed together).



Here's the scenario:  while driving to work, you run into the same traffic every day.  You stop, turn you hot engine off, and sit.  While your engine is off, the heat inside of it is killing the oil because it stops flowing, your battery is going to be drained because you probably won't sit there in silence - the radio will probably be on (oh yeah, and you'll need those cold cranking amps to restart your car, which is a big draw on the battery), and the shifter in automatic transmission cars are going to be put through quite an exercise.



Each car is made with planned obsolescence - they know that you can open and close your door "X" amount of times before the hinge or bushings fail.  If you put your starter, your battery, fuel pump and oil pump through that kind of work every day, your car will ultimately fail much, much sooner than planned.

However, should you get stuck in traffic from an accident or construction and know that you will be sitting for extended amounts of time, then I fully agree that we should do what we can to conserve & shut our car off...

Isn't all this really a matter of common sense?



</answer>

Insurers aren't that stupid

<answer from>

</answer from>



<answer>



yes insure in their name but you as named driver,



Will still cost an arm and leg though.  Insurance might be twice as much as car



costs.



</answer>



<answer from>



no answerer details



</answer from>



<answer>



You are legally not allowed to put your parents as the main driver(s) and you as a named driver.  This practice is called "fronting" and is completely illegal.



Your best bet is to get the insurance in your name, (as you must) and put 1 or both parents on YOUR insurance.  This can dramatically reduce your premiums with some insurance companies.



</answer>

<question>



Why diesel cars not so available in the USA.  I want one.  They have phenomenal mileage?



I rented a diesel in Europe and the tank stayed on full even though I was doing some solid driving for 5 days.  These are better than electric cars for mileage.



</question>



<answer from>



joeschmo...
Member since: 15 May 2007
Total points: 17515 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Other - Cars & Transportation



New York City



</answer from>



<answer>



Most European diesel cars do not meet the U.S. Environmental Protection Agency's Tier2 emissions standards.  That's why most of those cars are not allowed to be sold in the United States.

Only a few that meet the EPA's standards made by VW or Mercedes are sold here.  http://www.fueleconomy.gov/feg/compx2008...

The ones that are EPA-compliant have decent but not spectacular fuel economy, and keep in mind on average diesel fuel costs 80 cents more per gallon than gasoline in the United States.



</answer>



<answer from>



chuckles...
Member since: 30 June 2008
Total points: 6360 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



They sold some for a while in the late 70s. The quality was so bad that they tirned everyone off. They could not handle the extremely cold weather and very long driving times in America.

Plus, most of  them do not meet the pollution control requirements. They are dirtier than gasoline cars. Also, diesel fuel costs more than gasoline, which wipes out your savings anyway.

My daughter went on a trip in a diesel Golf across the USA in January a few years ago. There was no way that car was going to start in Fargo ND in January at 44 below. They had to abandon it.



</answer>

<question from>

</question from>



<question>



Driving test tomorrow, any advice or words of wisdom?



I'm sitting my first driving test tomorrow. Not sure what to expect, please share your experiences, good or bad! Thanks:)



</question>



<additional details>



Haha, yes, very witty!



</additional details>



<answer from>



Amazingw...
Member since: 20 February 2008
Total points: 8604 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



On your lesson before the test, drive around the streets close-ish to the test centre.  You want to be looking out for things that could affect your drive - such as road works, diversions, broken traffic lights, temporary traffic lights, massive lorries blocking roads, etc.  You don't want to be driving along and wham, you're faced with an un-expected thing which may flummox you.

Before you get in the car (for your test), you do the sight test, you then do "Show me, Tell me", you will be asked a few questions, such as "How do you change a tyre", "Where is the oil", "What goes in here?" - you'll be asked to pop the bonnet open for this.

You then close the bonnet, in the car, the examiner will adjust their mirrors and then say "Drive on when you'r ready, upon leaving the Test Centre, turn left / right / straight ahead until I tell you otherwise".

So, after a few minutes you'll turn into quieter streets and be asked to pull over.  You'll be told that you'll be doing one of the manovers, reverse round corner / turn in the road / parallel parking.  You have two of these to do.



Sometimes you get the Emergency Stop too, but they will forewarn you of this, not everyone gets them though.

After the manovers, back off out to the busier roads.  For me, I was made to drive along a 60mph twisty country lane and got a minor fault for going too slow (they can fault you for "un-due hesitancy").

After about 40mins, you are back at the test centre. Done and dusted.

Make sure you have reversed round every type of corner imaginable, not all corners are perfect right angles, they tested me on a wide, sweeping, downward corner !!



With mirrors, the examiner will adjust the tiny mirror near the rear view mirror.  This means they can see your eyes move to the mirrors so no need for exaggerated head / eye movements.

If your examiner jolts forward when you break, they are giving a subtle hint you are breaking too hard.  My instructor also told me that some examiners will give a gentle cough if you are dawdling about pulling out of a junction when they can see there is plenty of space.

Examiners don't want to fail people, they have to complete lots of paperwork explaining the reason they failed someone yet with a pass, it's just that piece of paper they hand you at the end when they shake your hand.



Take a deep breath, don't rush into things and don't take any herbal relaxant drops if you've never taken them before - you could end up like a friend of mine and be zombie-fied!!

Good luck!



</answer>

<answer>



Hiya,  I am an ex driving instructor so I can help.  The best advice I can give you is once you get into the test centre, sit down and relax.  Go in to your test with an open mind.  Feel positive put at the same time not over confident or negative.  Most test candidates feel they are going to get an examiner with grey hair and clipboard!  This is not usually the case anymore.  Much younger examiners have been recruited.  Always remember that you will not be doing anything on test that you have not already done.  Finally a golden piece of advice, when doing the manoevoures, take your time and don't rush them.  The examiner is not expected to see an experience driver/expert but someone who is a new driver who can drive safely.  Best of luck.  James



</answer>

<answer from>



CDT
Member since: 25 March 2008
Total points: 1630 (Level 3)



Contributing In:



Insurance & Registration



</answer from>



<answer>



There's already some excellent advice here, so all I wanted to add was this;

The examiner will understand that you're nervous and won't expect you to be perfect. As long as you drive in the way your instructor has taught you and don't do anything that could be construed as unsafe or dangerous, you should be fine.



You don't need to whizz your head around in an exaggerrated manner to prove you are looking in your mirrors or anything like that  - the examiner knows whether you're using your mirrors correctly anyway and remember it's not just about physically looking - it's about correctly interpreting and acting on what you SEE that counts.

The most important piece of advice I can give is to listen carefully to what you are asked to do and to CONCENTRATE fully for the whole drive...don't let your concentration slip or start thinking about what you've aleady done, you must think only about what you are being asked to do at that moment in time, nothing else.

If you think you've made a mistake, let it go and don't dwell on it - the chances are that it's not as bad as you think :) More people fail through lapses of concentration caused by fretting about something they feel they didn't do perfectly and letting this affect the rest of their drive, than by the original fault...

If you're nervous, then heres a link to a website with some tips of dealing with Test Day Nerves - http://www.squidoo.com/practical-driving...

GOOD LUCK :)



Source(s):



I own and run a UK driving school

http://www.squidoo.com/practical-driving...



</answer>

<question>

<question>



My tire pressure light keeps coming on, it is because of the rims?



Everytime I turn on my vehicle (2007 Dodge Nitro) a light turns on and keeps beeping. It is the symbol that indicates that on one or more of my tires, my air pressure is low. Could this be because I have put new tires and 22" rims on all 4? I checked the pressure with a guage and it was good. Could the sensor be broken?



</question>



<answer from>



Evil O
Member since: 08 July 2007
Total points: 2586 (Level 4)



Contributing In:



Volkswagen



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



The sensors the tire pressure monitoring system is looking for are attached to the valve stems.  Did you put those in your new rims?

If you transfered them to your new rims, and your spare tire has the correct air pressure.  Then there is a good chance one of the sensors got damaged.

You will need a factory scanner to tell you which one is bad.



Source(s):



Mechanic 20 years



</answer>

<question>



What is the difference between air conditioning and air recirculation?



Air conditioning costs more but what exactly is the difference between the two systems? Is air recirculation effective at cooling or heating the vehicle interior on hot or cold days?



</question>



<answer from>



Andrew
Member since: 11 December 2006
Total points: 1673 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Air Recirculation and/or the Max A/C button do the same thing. They close off the vents that let fresh air into the car from the base of the windshield.

Air Recirculation should be combined with A/C usage, which is why on some cars (older domestic US cars in particular) it's labled Max A/C.  Air Recirc setting helps the air conditioner system by recirculating already cooler air through the system, rather then trying to take warmer outside air and cool it down.



You can use it with heat, but on some cars (older Japanese and German cars in the US) they aren't set-up to run the A/C compressor in defrost mode.  Any moisture at all will cause your windows to fog, ALL OF THEM,  unless you also turn on the A/C button too.

A/C doesn't really cost much more then 1 mpg on most cars. For me personally, I'll use A/C whenever I can. My comfort is worth more then 1 mpg.



</answer>

<question>



What do all these numbers mean on my car tires?



195/60 R15
88H
M+S

Those are all of the numbers I was able to obtain off of my uniroyal tires on my car. Can anyone explain exactly what these numbers mean and what size rim these tires will fit on?
I want to get new rims and i want 16 inch, but since my tires are new i don't want to have to buy new tires for the new rims. Anyway that i can use these tires on a 16inch rim?
Any additional info when shopping for car parts and good/cost effective websites are greatly apprieciated.
Thanks in advance!



</question>



<additional details>



Would you reccomend getting all new rims and tires or trying to find a new rim that would fit that tire? The tires are only about 4 months old. Are there any places where you can trade in slightly used tires for new ones?



</additional details>



<answer from>



Mark F
Member since: 03 December 2006
Total points: 25750 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Maintenance & Repairs



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



"195" is the section width of the tire in millimeters.  This is measured about half-way down the sidewall.  It is NOT a measure of the width of the tread.

"60" is the section height or aspect ratio which measures the height of the tire from the bead to the top of the tread as a percentage of the section width - in other words, the sidewall is 60% of 195mm.

"R" means the tire is of radial construction

"15" is the diameter of the wheel the tire is mounted on.  Obviously the tire won't fit on a 14 or a 16" diameter wheel if the hole in the middle is 15 inches.

"88" is the load index of the tire and indicates how much weight the tire can carry at specific inflation pressures.

"H" is the speed rating of the tire and indicates the speed the tire can sustain under test conditions.

If you want to buy 16" wheels you will need tires with a 16-inch diameter hole in the middle, not a 15-inch one.  Specifically a tire sized 205/50R16 87H (or any higher speed rating such as V, W or Y) is what you will be looking for.  A tire this size will require an inflation pressure increase of 1 psi over what is recommended for your 15" tires due to the lower load index.



M+S isn't part of the tire size.  It is a label applied to show the tire meets RMA requirements for service in Mud and Snow conditions - an all-season tire.

www.tirerack.com is a great place to shop for tire and wheel packages.



Source(s):



I work in the tire and wheel industry



</answer>



<asker's comment>



Exactly the information i wanted to know! Thanks!



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



A Pcarman
Member since: 19 August 2008
Total points: 556 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>

They even mount and balance the tires on the wheels and ship them to you. They have great tech support that can answer all your questions about what sizes will fit your particular application.

<question>



1.3CDTI pulling 950KG ?



i have asked a similar question before, but this time i have weights etc available!

the van is a combo CDTI 
75bhp - 170ft lb Torque

the trailer/load combination is 950KG
will the van be able to tow that no problems?
also will it be ok to pull it up a small ramp? (boat launch ramp)



reason im asking is because im about to buy a boat, but i need to be sure, or almost sure my van will be able to take it back out of the water!

if not any one got any other suggestions or tips? Winch the trailer up the ramp? reverse with the trailer attached to front of van?



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



You need to check your owners manual for your van to see what it is rated for.



If you over load it, you can damage or cause failure to your engine, brakes, auto transmission or clutch, axles, tires, trailer hitch and your suspension.



Do you really want to wreck your van AND your boat because your brakes overheated and failed?



</answer>

<question>



Advice on driving a Van?



Passed my driving test in June this year. Just got through a job interview for a Delivery Driver position at Waitrose, will have to pass a van assessment this week. The van they use are 3.5 tonne (LWB) Mercedes-Benz Sprinters. Any advice on driving these vans since the biggest thing I've driven so far is a Honda Accord. Plus, anybody know what the assessor looks out for on the run, and is it aas strict as a driving test? Any help will be very much apprieciated.



</question>



<answer from>



hunter7
Member since: 15 April 2007
Total points: 244 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



The assessment will not be as strict as if you were performing your driving test. 
They are Waitrose partners, not specially qualified driving professional examiners. However, they will need to be confident of your driving ability. They will check that you are able to drive the vehicle in a safe and considerate manner. 
There are two pieces of advice I would give when driving these. One, please remember the vehicle is wider and longer than what you normally drive. You should therefore allow more room to make turns than you normally do, without hitting curbs on a left turn! (Something I have been prone to do in this vehicle... I normally drive a small hatchback!). Provided you compensate for the extra width you will be fine.



Two, the gear stick will probably be located on the centre column, there will be an instinct to "reach down" to change gears, only to realise the gearstick is higher up than what you imagine.



The examiner will ask you to make a reverse maneuver, there is nothing wrong in asking them about distances behind during this.  Good luck.



Source(s):



Waitrose Partner



</answer>



<asker's comment>



Some sound advice from many of you,

Thank you.



</asker's comment>

<answer from>



daftoldw...
Member since: 23 September 2006
Total points: 2659 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



tell the assessor you have never driven a van. 
Make sure you use your mirrors often.
Observe speed limits.
If you can, tell the assessor why you do things. for example if you are going along a narrow street with parked cars, say that you are slowing down incase a child should run out.
Comment on any hazards you may observe. can be as simple as a bush blocking a driveway so the user has to nudge out to see.
Basically drive carefully, dont take risks but make good progress and for goodness sake, keep a good distance from the vehicle in front as most accidents are caused by not enough braking distance.
The assessor isnt there to test you, just observe and maybe comment on how you could improve your skills. He/she is aware that you are a qualified driver.
Dont worry about it, assessors are human.



Source(s):



Driver assessor and trainer for 15 years



</answer>



<answer from>



Richard C
Member since: 09 February 2008
Total points: 462 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



Hi, another point not mentioned so far is to be careful of the length of the van when turning left, i.e. from a side turning on to a Main Road. It's all too easy to put the rear wheel up on the kerb, not too impressive when someone's assessing you! 
Also, watch your mirrors carefully whilst negotiating roundabouts.
Hope this helps, and good luck!



Source(s):



Mercedes-Benz Sprinter driver for 7 years!



</answer>



<answer from>



david m
Member since: 25 June 2008
Total points: 565 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



Daftold... answers are very sound advice,could I add a few more?



Ask for a `cockpit` drill before your assessment,some of the controls are going to be in different places to the Accord.Allow for the length of the vehicle especially when turning left as you will need more nearside room to avoid clipping the kerb. Make sure your mirrors are adjusted to your driving position,check this before you move away,no interior rear view mirror is going to be one of the biggest things you`ll have to get used to. As with the other answer,the assessor wants to know that you drive safely and realises that a good driver quickly gets used to a new vehicle. If you had time I`d hire a similar size van for 1/2 day to see what it feels like.



Good luck,take care of your driving,too many bad ones out there.



Source(s):



Ex London bus driver,driving instructor,taxi driver.



</answer>



<answer from>



michael7...
Member since: 06 January 2007
Total points: 354 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



It has been mentioned already but the most thing you need to watch is the increased lenght of the vehicle. Allow plenty room when cornering and turning left or right. When turning left especially, keep a close eye on your left wing mirror as you could easily clip a kerb, or worse still, a parked car. It might sound stupid, but don't forget the extra height. I'v seen many vans with damaged roofs where the driver must have tried to get under a 2 mitre height restriction. I assume the assessor knows you have no van driving experience and will allow for this. After 2 days it will be second nature. I don't know what he'll look for but you should try and drive "his" van with respect.



Source(s):



350,000 miles driven in vans.



</answer>

I taught a woman to drive, and she bought a car. This happened one night, his neighbour went to the shops, a space of 5 minutes, but his space had been taken. <answer>



hmm i have a slightly different answer to the others but here goes!

If you visit http://www.direct.gov.uk/en/TravelAndTra... this is the Highway Code online rules 238 - 252 on waiting and parking.

Rule 243 states
"do not stop or park in front of an entrance to a property" however, this is not a legal requirement or law (must not) it is just a rule (do not/should not) and as the introduction to the highway code states
"failure to comply with the other rules of the Code will not, in itself, cause a person to be prosecuted, The Highway Code may be used in evidence in any court proceedings under the Traffic Acts" http://www.direct.gov.uk/en/TravelAndTra...

If your neighbour doesn't use her drive, ask her if you can park across it slightly - even though technically, you wouldn't be breaking the law if you did - you might just p*ss her off a little, so I would suggest asking her politely first!!

good luck

<answer from>



Old Man Dirt
Member since: 23 April 2008
Total points: 6090 (Level 5)



Contributing In:



Maintenance & Repairs



</answer from>



<answer>



Many concept cars from auto shows (especially the GM brands) are only mock-ups. Many times GM has not even put an engine in them and the parts you see are  artist renditions of  what they think will sell the car.
So many concept  cars are non working.
Those that are, many times  are broken up and never sold. However some find the way to company museums or a designers private collections.
Even prototypes are very often destroyed. Pro-types are test beds for design and test function.
There are private concept  care that come up for sale, which unless it is a known car builder (like say Saleen) are not worth the money in many cases.



</answer>



<answer from>



Twisted_...
Member since: 05 July 2006
Total points: 21738 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Motorcycles



</answer from>



<answer>



In 99% of cases no.  Concept cars are not for sale.

The very, very few that have ever been sold go for several millions of pounds, and end up in museums or private collections.



</answer>

Roy V

</answer from>



<answer>



Info for UK plated vehicles
====================

Back then, there wasn't a rollover year plate - they would just carry on issuing until they ran out of numbers. The three letter + 3 number format indicates that it was registered before 1963

The WML indicates that it was first registered in Middlesex.

Now if the photo of the car were available it might make it easier.



Source(s):



http://www.cvpg.co.uk/REG.pdf



</answer>



<answer from>



Tetchy Old Git
Member since: 30 June 2006
Total points: 2602 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



If memory serves me correctly, the first number plates with a dating sequence started on August 1st 1962. ie XYZ 123A, where A was the year. The car was therefore registered before that date, but it may be of any age in the photo. I'd focus on the car itself to get a rough idea of the age of the photo.

(This is assuming it's a UK registered car!)



</answer>



<answer from>



Neil
Member since: 16 June 2006
Total points: 11012 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



Because the plate does not have an age identifier (these were first introduced in 1962 in England, Scotland and Wales, and applied to all new cars by 1965, but have still not been introduced in Northern Ireland), your best bet is to identify the car make and model, and then find someone who knows that model of car and can identify the age of it. You might be able to contact the owners club for the relevant make or model.

Another option is to see if the tax disc is visible on the car, as this will allow you to date the picture more accurately (the disc should have been valid at the time when the photo was taken, but you can't tell how old the car was when the photo was taken). You may need to magnify part of the photo for this.



</answer>

<answer>



Mine does this for ABS and Traction Control.
It is to tell you it's working OK.
If the light stays on it is a fault warning.

<answer from>



Britblok...
Member since: 07 January 2008
Total points: 1437 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



As the others have said, the ABS light is a function check - if it stays on the ABS is faulty and you need to have it checked - no, they're not showing off, if the ABS is faulty you could have an accident that could have been avoided.



The temperature is handy if it's getting close to freezing - the obvious reason being ice on the roads. My car gives a little warning sound as well when the temperature gets close to freezing. So - it's true to say we could live without it and the car would work ok without it, but it's not really showing off - it's just another safety feature.



We could, for example, probably find our way around at night without headlights, but it's kinda handy to have them!



</answer>

<answer from>
Member since: 17 September 2006

Contributing In:



Drawing & Illustration

<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



depending on the size(width)of car, you should be at least aligned with the tread wear line on road, if smaller vehicle then closer to center of your lane, basically suppose to be close to white line,



~if you have time, go out on empty road and park where you think you should be, then get out and look on passenger side how near you are to white line or side of roadway, then move to white line or side of paved edge, and check again with alignment through windows,



if no roads clear, use vacant shopping mall lot with lines to practice in,



┷┷┷┷┷┷┷┷┷┷ ║ ┷┷┷┷┷┷┷┷┷┷┃┋

Space to the Side:
Keep as much space as you can between yourself and oncoming vehicles. On a two-lane road, this means not crowding the centerline. In general, it is safest to drive in the center of your lane.

<original file>3101.html</original file>

<current file name>yqa_uk51661.txt</current file name>




<category>



Cars & Transportation



Other - Cars & Transportation



</category>



<question from>



Gutted
Member since: 13 August 2008
Total points: 149 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Is anyone here a lorry driver (in the UK)?



I'm thinking of becoming a lorry driver as I've heard that you can potentially earn good money.



There are loads of websites advertising 5-10 day training courses to pass your 'Class C' and'Class C& E' lorry driving tests.
They don't give prices but I've heard from people they rip you right off.



Has anyone ever used one of these companies to pass? How much did it cost roughly and how much do you earn on an average week now you are qualified?



A friend has said of mine told me that if you get a job with Robert Wiseman they pay for your test but you have to work for them for 2 years, does anyone have any more info on this?



I need a better paid job!
Many Thanks



</question>



<answer from>



Mostin46
Member since: 30 September 2008
Total points: 116 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



A training course should only last 5 days, typically you start on a Monday and take your test on the following Friday. I did my HGV Class II, now called / known as LGV " C " in May 2004, it cost me 600 quid. In Feb this year I did my LGV C+E ( formerly known as HGV class 1 ), this was more expensive, at 900 quid, inc the test fee. The question to which I don't know the answer, is, how much it will cost you to train directly for attaining the C + E licence ? However, most drivers would recommend you take your C licence ( rigid vehicles ) first of all, and gain some experience, before taking your C + E . Re. wages, all companies pay about the same ( unless you get a job in a more specialised area eg. chemical tankers or car transportation ), but the wages will depend on whether you do " nights out " ie you sleep in the lorry and are away from home, if so, for how many nights ?  Also depends on things such as does the company pay you bonuses such as a load bonus  ?  whether your on a basic plus overtime, or just an hourly rate and so on. You can expect to earn anywhere from 300 quid a week to 450 quid after tax ie. in your pocket.(but to earn 450 Im away from home Mon until Friday and you will work @ 60 hours a week) . The difference between our artic drivers and rigid drivers is only about 1500 quid a year.



</answer>



<asker's comment>



Thankyou, brilliant answer.



</asker's comment>

<question>



Motorhome, cover during winter or not.?



I am trying to decide if I should fully cover my new motorhome during the winter months. I have heard it can cause more troubles than benefits. Opinions Please



</question>



<answer from>



casual looker
Member since: 02 October 2008
Total points: 110 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Inside storage is a sensible option, it costs a bit but the damage done by leaving it outside may well cost a lot more or even damage it beyond repair. If your motorhome is brand new, I would surmise it cost a considerable amount of money and as such I would protect my investment they best way I could, I guess this really goes for a used one also. Save £500 on storage but suffer £2000 worth of damage. Motorhomes are an expensive piece of equipment which usually hold their value, but any damage however minor will affect resale value if and when you come to sell it. A well looked after motorhome will always draw top resale money.



</answer>



<asker's comment>



Very informative, thanks



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



smartblo...
Member since: 29 April 2008
Total points: 10202 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



If you must leave it outside a breathable cover is a good bet. A cover that does not come into direct contact with the roof or body is even better, which is why most owners prefer to put theirs in a shed or garage.



My neighbour does not have the luxury of a storage building so he just builds an enclosure around his for the winter and covers it with construction plastic. The cover is vented and is supported at key points by struts attached to the motorhome.



</answer>



<answer from>



dumbhill...
Member since: 07 November 2007
Total points: 878 (Level 2)



</answer from>

As long as you have winterized it i would not worry about covering the hole thing.If there is a question about the roof leaking cover it I know a lot of people re caulk the roof every year before winter hits

K  Baby
Member since: 04 September 2008

<question>



People who drive exotic cars should pay more when they fill up their tank..Agree?



Because sports/exotic cars consume much more fuel, should they pay more when they fill up?



</question>



<answer from>



rataflim...
Member since: 28 August 2008
Total points: 1264 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



they do pay more.



1. exotic cars typically need higher-octane grade fuels, which are more expensive.
2. they stop at the gas station more often.



hence, they pay more. you can't price it higher for them. it's the same gas  that was made regardless of what car it goes into. it's like asking them to pay more than you do for their mcdonald's value meal. you don't punish them for being rich. they just have to pay more taxes, that's it.



</answer>



<answer from>



scott h
Member since: 26 July 2006
Total points: 433 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



if you mean exotic in the sense of ferrari,lambo  and so forth  , they pay a gas guzzler tax when purchasing them new that well exceeds the price of a little more per gallon  on each fill up. Do some research i think you might feel bad them.



</answer>



<answer from>



Robert S
Member since: 11 January 2008
Total points: 142 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



Why should they ? The fuel consumption of a vehicle automatically penalises owners of vehicles which consume a lot of fuel because tax and duty on fuel is a massive proportion of the cost. Sports and exotic cars are not necessarily more uneconomical than even large family saloons and driving style is the biggest factor in poor fuel consumption. There are probably arguments for very strict safety checks on performance cars but straight taxes on a 'type' of vehicle are a very unfair way of influencing buying decisions. Perhaps the fools who buy Prius type vehicles should be penalised for being so naive in thinking that they have made an environmentally sound choice. Whole life environmental impact assessment is the only way to fairly link product purchases with some kind of tax.



</answer>



<answer from>



tricky
Member since: 25 March 2007
Total points: 25161 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Nissan



</answer from>



<answer>



I tend to drive sporty cars and i have to fill up with higher octane fuel,and pay more on insurance and taxes.
But to be fair i get 17mpg driving slow but there are plenty of trucks and cars and lorries etc which get less mpg than me and even someone driving a smaller car fast all the time.
Driving a sporty car makes you take it out less due to fuel cost so better for the enviroment,than someone with higher mpg driving all the time.
Owning a sporty cars restricts you driving long journeys so is on the road less.So to be fair its a punishment in its own right owning a sporty car.



</answer>

I know they aren't halogen for the least. Pretty much they almost don't even look like normal lighting and only certain types of cars have them. I was just seeing if anyone knows exactly what im talking a about. I wish I could be more descriptive but im open to your answers. Thanks =)

<answer>

</answer>



<answer from>



rataflim...
Member since: 28 August 2008
Total points: 1264 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



they're high-intensity discharge or H.I.D. lighting systems. think if it as the fluorescent bulb and socket and your typical halogen bulb is the incandescent bulb to house lights. it uses less power and shines brighter, but because they're a pretty new technology, they cost more. most high-end variants of cars and almost all luxury brands have these installed out of the factory, but they usually don't have the purple-ish tinge (basically because the purple-ish ones aren't safe anymore, they're too bright and actually are more of a bother to the oncoming cars than lighting up the road in front of you). you can buy those as aftermarket parts, though.



</answer>

<answer>

Windshield Non-reflective tint is allowed on the top 4 inches of the windshield.



Front Side Windows Any darkness can be applied 4" inches from the top of the window.



Back Side Windows Any darkness can be used.



Rear Window Any darkness can be used.

Back Side Windows Must not be more than 35% reflective.

<question>



What type of gas is better?



I have a chrysler 300 I currently put the regular gas in it(the cheapest one) I can afford the other ones I just dont know what kind im supposed to put on Can eny one explain it to me



</question>



<answer from>



Larry E
Member since: 20 November 2007
Total points: 13908 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Maintenance & Repairs



</answer from>



<answer>



If the car runs good on it it's fine.



</answer>



<answer from>



John Paul
Member since: 17 July 2006
Total points: 46967 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Nissan



</answer from>



<answer>



Consult your cars owners manual. The higher the octane the slower the fuel burns. Slow burning generates more heat. Some cars need High octane fuel High Compression Superchargers and Turbocharging Corvette, GTO's Nissan 350Z Maximas Muranos all reccomend high octane. Running those cars on low octane will cause spark knock ping and dieseling. Most modern cars adjust for fuel to some extent so running low octane can cause less gas milage. I suspect your Chrysler 300 runs fine on 87 octane.



Source(s):



Nissan GTR master technician



</answer>



<answer from>



UCANTCME
Member since: 16 April 2007
Total points: 135697 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Maintenance & Repairs



Safety



Other - Cars & Transportation



</answer from>



<answer>



Most vehicles require regular "87" octane gas and using a higher octane rating will most likely not give you better fuel mileage.



While higher octane gas is required in some vehicles, its use is to prevent "knocking or pinging" within the engine of your vehicle.



Using a much higher octane rated gas when it is not required can cause the fuel to not burn completely.



This results in poorer fuel economy.



Some vehicles require this higher octane gas and not using it can leave one with undesirable results.



As always, consult your owners manual.



Source(s):



316



</answer>



<answer from>



Muledanc...
Member since: 30 August 2006
Total points: 33684 (Level 7)



</answer from>



<answer>



If you do not experience preignition ("spark knock") then drop one grade of fuel and use the entire tank.  When your engine develops the characteristic rattle of pre-ignition, go UP one grade level of fuel.



If you have the owner's manual or can ask a nearby Chrysler dealer, go with factory recommendations.



</answer>

<question>



Can you do asphalt work on a rainy day?



It is supposed to rain after noon today.  My fiance works at the airport (small for personal jets, crop dusters, etc... not for commercial airlines).  They are making 2 new strips and the asphalt was supposed to be laid down today.  I just wonder if he will get to come home early or if if he wont work because you can (or cant) do asphalt on a day that there will be 90% chance of rain.  He just left...and Im hoping he comes home! :)

<answer from>



kelly_f_...
Member since: 22 September 2006
Total points: 17265 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>

</answer>



<answer from>



chicky-p...
Member since: 16 March 2008
Total points: 110 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



You can, but the job will be extrememly dodgy.
Not something you'd want at an airport?



</answer>



<answer from>



maddogs
Member since: 15 September 2008
Total points: 19 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



forget it go to the pub.



Source(s):



thats what Im doing



</answer>



<answer from>



Robsteri...
Member since: 05 April 2008
Total points: 4572 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



Unless the rain is really torrential then it's no problem.



Hot asphalt is laid using wet rollers and the water just boils off. In heavy rain it just means that the rollers don't need spraying. Torrential rain though can cool down the asphalt too quickly as it's being laid, but even then there are ways to adjust the mixture, laying speed and temperature to offset most of the problems.



Road gangs generally will do all they can to complete the job, when I used to do it no laying meant no paying!



Source(s):



Been there, done that, got the scars to show for it...



</answer>

Chris B
Member since: 12 July 2008
Total points: 8 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



What the most fun you have had in a tuned car?



like burning out or drifting .



</question>



<answer from>



Vipassan...
Member since: 24 July 2007
Total points: 15820 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Buying & Selling



Maintenance & Repairs



Other - Cars & Transportation



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Everytime I get to set someone straight about a tuned car, is the most fun.



For example, putting a turbo on a car, is not tuning it. Changing the wheels, is not tuning it. An intake, exhaust, etc, is not tuning.



All these things simply modify or replace original parts.



Unless the car has sat on a dyno for weeks, as you adjust every bit of fuel mapping, or suspension rebound, or camber angle, etc.......the car isn't tuned.



And then I laugh as the poor kids realize that they're complete tools and don't understand the first thing about cars.



Source(s):



I own several tuned vehicles. My pride and joy is my 1988 Supra Turbo, which competes in autocross racing. Has taken 3 years and several engine rebuilds to get the optimal performance.



</answer>



<asker's comment>



thanks



</asker's comment>

<original file>13646.html</original file>

<current file name>yqa_uk63376.txt</current file name>




<category>



Cars & Transportation



Other - Cars & Transportation



</category>



<question from>



james p
Member since: 02 May 2008
Total points: 91 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Which is better when thinking about fast cars?



A Bugatti Veyron, An Ascari A10, A Caparo T1 or An Ariel Atom



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



A Bugatti Veyron top speed is 253 mph, and goes 0-62 in 2.5 seconds.



An Ascari A10 top speed is 220 mph, and goes 0-60 in 2.8 seconds.



A Caparo T1 top speed is 205 mph, and goes 0-62 in 2.5 seconds.



An Ariel Atom top speed is 140 mph, and goes 0-60 in 2.7 seconds.



So those are the facts but the  "Ultimate Aero TT" is the fastest production car with a top speed of 256 and 0-60 in 2.7 seconds



</answer>

Member since: 08 May 2007
Total points: 378 (Level 2)



</question from>



<question>



What is the average cost per mile of petrol for a Vauxall Corsa?



a 1.0 litre version. say with a fuel price of 110 pence per litre. i guess all thats needed is the miles per gallon to work that one out, or how many litres per mile of fuel does it drink?



</question>



<answer from>



J G
Member since: 19 May 2008
Total points: 20854 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Maintenance & Repairs



Other - Cars & Transportation



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



The 1.0 version should average around 50mpg - the book figures vary from 48mpg to 53mpg dependant on model & age, so at 110p per litre, or £4.90 per gallon, equates to about 10p per mile (9.8 to be precise).
This equates to 17.7 litres/100km, or 0.177l / km. (0.285l / mile)



</answer>



<asker's comment>



.



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



Aged Biker
Member since: 25 September 2008
Total points: 214 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



This a bit of a "how long is a bit of string" question.
Making the assumption that you are in the UK, depends on who's driving it, how they're driving, where they're driving, what time of the year,etc.
A good guide, particularly if you are to do lots of urban driving, is to take the Govt. published consumption figures and HALVE them.
This should give you a fairly accurate figure for what the car will achieve out there in the real world, as opposed to on a test track or in some experimental laboratory.



Source(s):



In over 40 years of motoring, I have never achieved manufacturer's claimed figures, in any car.



</answer>

<answer from>



ravii_aa...

Total points: 1758 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



I agree with jeff. he is right marketing involves 4 P's



Price,



Place,
Promotion,
Product.



BUT



Before we go ahead, let me tell you something.



Marketing is Vital aspect in any Industry (whether it is service Industry, Manufacturer, etc..) it is very important to have marketing aspect to run any industry smoothly without any hurdles.



But what is Marketing ?



Most people think that marketing is only about the advertising and/or personal selling of goods and services. Advertising and selling, however, are just two of the many marketing activities.



"Marketing is the process of planning and executing the conception, pricing, promotion, and distribution of ideas, goods, services, organizations, and events to create and maintain relationships that will satisfy individual and organizational objectives"



in simpler word marketing is "process"; marketing involves researching, promoting, selling, and distributing your products or services.



scope of marketing



i think if you analyse yourself with the above answer you will understand the scope of marketing yourself.



Bye



I think i have helped you



Ravi. Achary

<question>



What does it mean when a car is in neutral?



I dont drive, so I dont know anything about cars.



</question>



<answer from>



likesfem...
Member since: 15 May 2008
Total points: 4948 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



The transmission is not in any gear. It's like your mountain bike with no chain on it...you can peddle all you want, but you won't get anywhere.



</answer>



<answer from>



Super Chris
Member since: 10 December 2006
Total points: 199 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



It means the car isn't in gear. That means the engine is running but it is not touching any gears so it won't effect the wheels.



</answer>



<answer from>



ICANANSW...
Member since: 04 February 2008
Total points: 171 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



It means the cars transmission is not in gear. This means the car is free and moves on it own weght or if someone or something pushes it .It will likely not move in a flat surface but it will on an incline.



</answer>



<answer from>



timtams0...
Member since: 06 October 2008
Total points: 121 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



it means that the gearbox is not engaged to a gear.



example if your in a manual car then you take it out of gear and let the clutch out if you rev the engine with the handbrake off (assuming your on flat ground) you wont go anywhere. as soon as you take it out od "neutral" and put it in first release the clutch and press the gas it moves forward. In an auto N is neutral and D,1,2,3,R are your driving gears.



</answer>



<answer from>



gib
Member since: 24 October 2006
Total points: 2351 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



When the car is in neutral, it means the shifter is in the center position that takes the transmission out of all pulling gear positions and the engine will run but the car will not move. In reality, the power from the  engine is disconnected from the rear wheels by the transmission and the car will not move but the engine will continue running. This is called NEUTRAL.



Source(s):



me , an old mechanic



</answer>



<answer from>



gfgfkj f
Member since: 06 October 2008
Total points: 141 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>

visit
http://automobilesltd.blogspot.com

<question>



Does leaving the car running waste gas?



I was wondering if leaving the car running while waiting for someone saves more gas rather than turning it off and waiting for that person?



I've always been the one to turn it off, because I feel like my car needs a rest from the heat and because it seems to me that gas is being used even while it's idle.



</question>



<answer from>



pingme
Member since: 18 November 2007
Total points: 2953 (Level 4)



Contributing In:



Land Phones



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



For a typical engine, it takes more gas to start an engine than it does to let it idle for about 3 minutes.



So, for saving fuel, if the errand is less than 3 minutes, then let it idle, if its longer, shut it down.  In cold weather you want to add about 50% to this, so about 4 1/2 minutes.



Also, starting an engine is one of the hardest things you can do to it, so a lot of engine start/shut-offs can actually make the engine wear faster.



As far as "rest from the heat", your engine is running the coolest when coolant (anti-freeze) is running through it.  Shutting the engine off stops the circulation of coolant and there is actually a rise in temperature right after you shut off the engine, again hard on it if this happens a lot.



</answer>



<asker's comment>



gave explanation as to when it is okay to turn off engine and why one solution saves more energy than the other. even goes as far as weather conditions (winter) and specific times it's okay to turn off the engine rather than leaving it runnning. a really great answer. thank you.



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



alok j
Member since: 25 October 2006
Total points: 96 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



Yes , it does wastes the gas. How ever if you feel you may need to move WITHIN 2-3 mins ( say: you are waiting for a  small trian to cross at the railways cross) then its wise to keep the engine running because it will waste more gas once you switch off the car and re-run it.



But... But, hold on if you find yourself  waiting fo some one or in a situation where you have to wait more than 2-3 min



, its better to switch off your car. Its nice to see people concerned and willing to save ennergy.



cheers

</answer>

</question from>



<question>



Why do we call it GASOLINE if it's a liquid?



I'm not asking about the name Petroleum, i want to know why the word "gasoline" is used to refer to that substance, when it isn't a gas, its a liquid.



Thanks :)



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



The name is actually entirely made up. Before we started running cars on the stuff, it was just an annoying by-product of the lamp-oil distillation process which was sold as a cleaning product. Some nineteenth-century marketer came up with the slick chemical-sounding name and bottled the stuff.



Source(s):



Wikipedia.



</answer>

I'm in London, I'm going to be using the DLR to travel to Heron Quays, and then the tube from there to the West End. Then I will be going to a bar somewhere else which will require using tube and DLR again. And then I'll be using DLR once again to get home. And does anyone know the rules of one day travel cards on oysters?
