<original file>5092.html</original file>

<current file name>yqa_uk70275.txt</current file name>




<category>



Cars & Transportation



Other - Cars & Transportation



</category>



<question from>



Banana
Member since: 24 May 2008
Total points: 2251 (Level 3)



</question from>



<question>



Travel card or oyster?



I'm in London, I'm going to be using the DLR to travel to Heron Quays, and then the tube from there to the West End. Then I will be going to a bar somewhere else which will require using tube and DLR again. And then I'll be using DLR once again to get home. I have an 18+ oyster card so I'm just wondering whether it would be better to buy a travel card on zones 1-2 for £4.90 or just simply use my oyster card. And does anyone know the rules of one day travel cards on oysters?



</question>



<answer from>



psycho bob
Member since: 21 August 2008
Total points: 510 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



Use your oyster card. Why pay more than you need?



</answer>



<answer from>



Fast Reg
Member since: 09 July 2008
Total points: 1001 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



I'd use the Oyster.



There are daily price caps on an Oyster which means that you should always be charged less than the cost of an equivalent one-day travelcard, irrespective of the number of journeys you make within a 24 hour period. The current off-peak price cap for zone 1-2 is £4.80, which is immediately 10p cheaper than the travelcard.



The other thing to bear in mind is that your journey might actually end up costing you less anyway if you use the Oyster. The total fares you incur in the day will be calculated on the journeys you actually make, so if you're not using it all that much you may never get anywhere near the price cap. With a travelcard you are automatically paying the maximum fare which may be wasteful if you're not extracting the maximum value from it.



Source(s):



http://www.tfl.gov.uk/tickets/oysteronli...



</answer>

<answer>



If they are permenant then they are probably traffic counters,- they take the numberplate and compare it against another reading further up the road to work out the traffic flow speed.



If they are temporary (or possibly permenant, we just have temporary ones where I stay) they are probably speed cameras, work the same as above, but are used for enforcement, working out your average speed over the distance between the two sets.  The ones we have here are blue and yellow though? so probably just the first thing.

<answer from>



Bardic
Member since: 21 November 2006
Total points: 42654 (Level 7)



</answer from>



<answer>



They're for traffic monitoring. The purpose is to feed traffic information back to the control centre to highlight slow moving traffic (i.e. traffic jams), and then to relay this information via various multimedia products.



That's where the info comes from when your local radio, for instance, says that "traffic is very heavy at such-and-such"



</answer>

<answer from>



Elmbeard
Member since: 07 March 2008
Total points: 4497 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



The harsh response (which must not be said!) is that if after 8 lessons you are still learning to steer, she is not a born teacher!



Methinks you need another teacher.



I learned to steer on my father's lap when I was about 8 and could not reach the pedals.  There was a private road we could go up and down.  I did the same for my son, when I still saw him.



I presume you have done the clutch control (avoiding kangarooing), hill starts using the handbrake, gear changing and when and how to brake.  A useful exercise for steering control is the slalom - weaving around obstacles.  Use cardboard boxes and a quiet private road to practise this.



Once you have mastered this, try the same in reverse.  Don't worry about squashing the boxes - just keep going back to the supermarket for more.  Bonfire night coming up soon, so by the time you get it right, there should be a goodly pile.



Once you can handle the controls without thinking too hard, you can then concentrate on thinking about road hazards. You this with a running commentary of everything you observe coming up in front or behind you.



As for not being a born driver - well, we are all pretty hopeless at it when we start.  The most dangerous driver though is the one who thinks he or she can do no wrong.



</answer>

bartman2...

</question from>



<question>



No Improvement in MPG?



I constantly see TV ads boasting some new car gets over 30 MPG. I am currently driving a manual 1999 Ford Escort ZX2, and it gets about 34 miles per gallon highway...Besides hybrids, how come there hasn't been much improvement in MPG since the late 90's?



</question>



<answer from>



hello
Member since: 21 May 2007
Total points: 5964 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



there are a number of things at play here.



first, last year the gov changed the rules for calculating MPG, so a car like the cobalt xfe which is rated at 37 mpg this year witha  stick would have been rated at 44 the year that your car was made.



second, b/c americans like speed and power, until 2 or 3 years ago most advancement was going to speed and had nothing to do with MPG's, but we're finally getting that turned around.



third, cars are heavier right now than they were then in part b/c they have to meet higher safety standards than the cars did the year yours was made.  advancement means weight, weight means MPG lost.



fourth,  cars are also heavier now b/c they are bigger.  the ford focus today is larger than the original ford taurus.  the honda civic is bigger than the old accords and the corolla is bigger than the old camry.  all of them still get better MPG's than the original larger car did.  a taurus today is as big as a crown vic was and still gets better mpg's.



</answer>



<asker's comment>



Yeah, I didn't think about weight and how much energy is lost to heat/vibration. Good call.



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



YamaMan
Member since: 26 October 2008
Total points: 178 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



People are addicted to power, and won't sacrifice it for mpg. The car companies only make cars that people will buy, and sometimes they mix performance with mpg, and get a car that lots of people will buy, with a little of each. If lots of people were looking for just a straight fuel efficient car then the car companies would make them. Also, there is only soo much mpg you can get out of a gasoline engine.



</answer>

Gasoline and Diesel........?



If oil is down and Gasoline is at $2.25 a gallon then why is diesel at $3.60? Is diesel that much more expensive to refine?



</question>



<answer from>



Reed L
Member since: 12 July 2006
Total points: 119 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



If ya can belive it......



Until several years ago, the average price of diesel fuel was usually lower than the average price of gasoline. In some winters when the demand for distillate heating oil was high, the price of diesel fuel rose above the gasoline price. Since September 2004, the price of diesel fuel has been generally higher than the price of regular gasoline all year round for several reasons. Worldwide demand for diesel fuel and other distillate fuel oils has been increasing steadily, with strong demand in China, Europe, and the U.S., putting more pressure on the tight global refining capacity. In the U.S., the transition to low-sulfur diesel fuel has affected diesel fuel production and distribution costs. Also, the Federal excise tax on diesel fuel is 6 cents higher per gallon (24.4 cents per gallon) than the tax on gasoline



</answer>



<asker's comment>



good answer



</asker's comment>

Diesel fuel and heating oil are the same thing. Seeing as cold weather is hitting many parts of the country, and many people did not fill their heating oil tanks this summer due to the increased prices, there  is a higher demand for diesel fuel / home heating oil that there is for gasoline right now.

<answer>



Yes, because it has to power a compressor to make the cold air. That in turn reduces gas mileage by making the engine work harder.



At low speeds it is generally more efficient to have the windows down.



HOWEVER - at high speeds it is more efficient than having the windows down (windows down makes a lot of drag), although still uses up more gas than having the windows up and the A/C off. (Use economy mode whenever possible, econ mode does not engage the compressor and therefore does not cause the engine to have to work harder and therefore use more gas)



</answer>

<answer>



any time you have a device that draws power from the engine it puts a higher work load on the engine, this in turn requires more fuel. this is one reason auto makers are working as much as possible to design cars lighter, the more weight you take off the less the motor has to work to move the car. this calculates out to better fuel mileage. Now a little bit about the EPA mileage, This is done in a controlled environment. They have All the accessories turned off and the car is running on a dyno with no wind resistance. at 55 mph for X number of miles. they then measure how much fuel is left in the tank (they start with a measured quantity) and calculate out how much fuel the car used. Based off this information they can then calculate out the MPG. now if they would drive the car as a normal person in the real world did, We would see mileage more common to what people calculate out. not the fictional number the EPA has developed.

<question>



Car radio inventor and year?



Additional info if possible.



</question>



<answer from>



UCANTCME
Member since: 16 April 2007
Total points: 140756 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Maintenance & Repairs



Safety



Other - Cars & Transportation



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



In 1929, American Paul Galvin, the head of Galvin Manufacturing Corporation, invented the first car radio.



The first car radios were not available from carmakers. Consumers had to purchase the radios separately.



Galvin coined the name "Motorola" for the company's new products combining the idea of motion and radio.



Source(s):



316



</answer>

<question from>



limonric...
Member since: 16 February 2008
Total points: 96 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



I have a Chrysler2007,  300.How important is it to warm your car up,during winter season?



My wife always in a rush to go to work.She would start the car, less than one minute.Drive off!



</question>



<answer from>



Mad Jack
Member since: 04 February 2006
Total points: 19390 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



Idling in the driveway to warm up the engine does nothing to prolong the life of the engine and wastes fuel.  Start and drive.



Modern cars are designed to be used in all weather conditions.  There is no reason to warm them up before you drive them, unless it is to defrost the windows.



There is no need to warm up the oil.  You are using multi viscosity oil in the engine.  If you are using the correct weight oil for the weather conditions, there is no need to warm the oil.  The engine has enough lubrication to be driven as soon as the oil pressure light goes out or there is oil pressure registering on the gage.  This is usually about 1 second on a well maintained engine.



By starting and driving, you will be doing more good than harm to your engine.  First, by idling in your driveway, you are causing normal wear and tear on your engine, but it is doing NOTHING.  Next, idling in your driveway makes your engine warm up more slowly.  When the engine is cold, the fuel injection adds additional fuel to the fuel / air mixture.  This causes more carbon buildup in the engine and gets more contaminates in the oil.  By starting and driving, your engine is up to operating temperature much faster and this rich fuel air mixture cycle is much shorter.



Now, the biggest reason not to idle in the driveway to warm up.  Think about it.  When you are idling in the driveway, you are getting ZERO miles per gallon.  It doesn't get any worse than that.



I live in northern Wisconsin were the winters can be quite harsh.  The temperature can get well below zero in the middle of winter.  I have been starting and driving my cars without warm-ups for decades without any problems.



My current cars are a 1995 Pontiac Grand Am and a 2000 Chevy Malibu.  I bought both of these cars new.  The only time I warm them up is to remove frost.  I have never had any problems from driving them like this.



</answer>



<answer from>



kelly_f_...
Member since: 22 September 2006
Total points: 21646 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



warmup time should be just enough to get the oil circulating to the valve gear. In summer that is 15 seconds!! In winter, with winter oil, take about 30 seconds. Then DRIVE OFF GENTLY. You still have to warm up the running gear. Your 10 minutes of idling is a collossal waste of money and causes much more wear on the car than driving off after 30 seconds. It also pollutes the air! The only exception is when your windows are frozen over; then you may neeed to run a little longer so you can see out.  your car warms up best and quickest while you are driving it. You can drive your car any time you want, you don't need to warm it up if it's in good condition. If it stalls when you put it in gear then you need to warm it up a few minutes.



Most people warm up their cars not because of the engine but it's SO cold they want the heater and defroster to be working when they hop in. There's nothing worse than driving in the cold with no heat.



</answer>



<answer from>



pyroline...
Member since: 16 November 2007
Total points: 150 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



yes at least wait till the rpm's are at a normak level.



</answer>

<answer from>



J G
Member since: 19 May 2008

Contributing In:



Maintenance & Repairs



Other - Cars & Transportation



</answer from>



<answer>



You'll find a massive difference - the steering will probably be heavier, the brakes poorer, it will probably stall more easily, the gearbox & clutch will feel very different.



Be prepared to spend the entire lesson just getting used to your car, and then practice regularly between lessons - don't switch back & forth between cars, you'll struggle to compensate for the differences.



</answer>

Alma K
Member since: 30 March 2007
Total points: 561 (Level 2)



</question from>



<question>



How are traffic lights' times coordinated?



Can two traffic lights in the same city (a relatively small city) have different timings? Who decides on these issues?



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



Traffic lights have three different types of coordination that dictate how they operate.  The first would be a simple timed controller which the local controller (usually a large box mounted on one of the traffic light polls at an intersection) has a timer that cycles the red and green lights at a set duration.  Also utilized are magnetic loop sensors embedded into the asphalt at the beginning of an intersection that will detect presence of cars and then eventually change the light from red to green. it can use a combination of these sensors, timers and programming dependent on time of day to determine which direction of traffic to prioritize to reduce congestion and delay.  Lastly there are coordinated lights that are part of a network of traffic lights in a specific area designed to stay green at the same time for example.  Lastly, the network of lights often in cities can be controlled by a central control center to control speeds or relieve congestion.  See link below for all of the different possibilities....



Source(s):



http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Traffic_lig...



</answer>






<original file>15241.html</original file>

<current file name>yqa_uk89130.txt</current file name>




<category>



Cars & Transportation



Other - Cars & Transportation



</category>



<question from>



Joe S
Member since: 02 June 2008
Total points: 314 (Level 2)



</question from>



<question>



Couple questions about Nitrous for my car?



For a 4 cylinder engine...



What preparation for the engine would be necessary if a 100 shot or 150 shot of nitrous was installed? I know if i just did a 50 shot that the stock parts can handle it.
(This would all be professionally installed and tuned)



Also do nitrous kits have everything needed or are more additional parts needed? such as this kit:
http://www.andysautosport.com/hyundai/20...



And last, can someone tell me more about wet and dry systems and the advantages and disadvantages of each?



No negative answers, just ones which will help. Thanks



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



For a 4 cylinder engine, you may want to go to forged pistons, connecting rods, and crankshaft if you are going over 100 horse power.  This is a must if you are going to 150+ horse power.



Also, you need to make sure the rest of the drive train is up to the task.  Your drive train is only as good as its weakest link.  Make sure the clutch, transmission, differential, and axles are up to the task.  Install stronger parts as necessary.



In higher horse power applications, a wet system is better.  A wet system adds more fuel to the mixture.  A dry system depends on the fuel injectors to add the additional fuel.  In higher horse power applications, a dry system may not be able to keep up with the additional fuel requirements and may lean the engine out.  This could cause burned valves or pistons.



The additional fuel from a wet system will make more power.



</answer>

<question>



How to driving up a hill in a Standard?



my grandparents got me a car but its a standard and i know how to drive one it just the hill part i know how to stop it just going again i always roll back and stall what do i do?
HELPPPP!!



</question>



<answer from>



rockbase
Member since: 27 January 2006
Total points: 8736 (Level 5)



Contributing In:



Motorcycles



</answer from>



<answer>



There are several ways to do this.



a) the backup plan... If you get stopped on a hill and are in a panic, put emergency brake on, Then when you go to take off, give it gas, ease clutch out and when you feel it start to pull forward, release the emergency brake.



b) Once stopped, keep the car in position by giving a little gas and also a little clutch out. not enough to move forward, but enough to stop roll back.



c) When you get better, You'll just stop like normal, and when lite changes you will take left foot off brake, push in clutch, give gas and slowly release clutch all very fast. it will be second nature and is the normal way of doing this.



You need to go to parking lot that has a hill in it, and on off hours practice. that's how i taught my wife and she does great !



</answer>



<answer from>



Freeyour...
Member since: 25 October 2008
Total points: 369 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



Well theres a couple of ways to handle this.
i live in the flattest state in the world but we have a bridge so i get my practice there.



Anyways the way i found easiest to learn was



hold the parking brake with the buttom pressed in and then pretend to go.
as in let the clutch out and add the gas when your confident that you have it so it wont roll back on you let the parking brake down and then just go.



i'd suggest you find a hill that isnt crowded so that you can practice with out hitting some car behind you.



</answer>



<answer from>



Grainov Truth
Member since: 26 July 2007
Total points: 15224 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



Then you "don't" know how to drive one.  This is one of those areas of skill.



Try using the handbrake to hold the car with one hand on it ready to release it slowly as the clutch grabs the engine more(slowing it down)- as you will have to add a bit of gas to increase engine speed higher than idle speed.



As you release the park brake you should feel the car move forward instead of backward and continue to stay running - instead of stalling.



Remember that control of the clutch pedal is what controls the contact between the engine and the wheels.



Slow release will keep the engine going until the wheels grab.



A quickly released clutch(or dropping the clutch) will either cause the wheels to spin or stall the engine.



You want to find the happy medium with minimal rollback.



And it varies on every hill (because they have different slopes).



Or try on lesser hills....stopping and starting.  It is very repetitive (but it is thru repetition that you learned to tie your shoelaces.)



Off road on gravel roads, do it where you don't hear the tires spin out in the gravel.



Later, this skill you learn here (of listening to the engine) will help you greatly in the winter snow as a "spinning tire gets no traction - whereas one that rolls slowly will pull you through".



I always think about it when coming to a stop on a hill.  It is not the preferred  place to stop for one and what am I carrying or towing at that time which adds additional weight to the starting power required to pull the car forward, and road conditions - dry pavement/wet/gravel/or snow.



Got to remember you want the tires to "turn or roll forward" not spin out.



Once you start spinning, you have lost the traction.



Source(s):



Driven nothing but standard. Car, motorcyle, semi rigs.



</answer>

<question from>



Dwayne B
Member since: 11 May 2007
Total points: 3777 (Level 4)



</question from>



<question>



Why do the British drive on the opposite side of the road than Americans?



And are there other countries that do this as well?



</question>



<answer from>



Rhymes with Orange
Member since: 05 July 2007
Total points: 2698 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



There are 75 countries that drive on the left (or as the British say, "The correct side of the street"), which include the UK, Japan, Jamaica and others.



Why do they drive on the left? Because that was the standard set by the Romans, who built the first roads in Europe



In fact, some (e.g. C. Northcote Parkinson) believe that ancient travellers on horseback generally rode on the left side of the road. As more people are right-handed, horsemen would thus be able to hold the reins with their left hands and keep their right hand free—to offer in friendship to passing riders or to defend themselves with swords, if necessary



The first legal reference in Britain to an order for traffic to remain on the left occurred in 1756 with regard to London Bridge. The Highway Act 1773 contained a recommendation that horse traffic should remain on the left and this is enshrined in section 78 of the Highway Act 1835.



In the late 1700s, a shift from left to right took place in countries such as the United States, when teamsters started using large freight wagons pulled by several pairs of horses. The wagons had no driver’s seat, so a postilion sat on the left rear horse and held his whip in his right hand. Seated on the left, the driver naturally preferred that other wagons pass him on the left so that he could be sure to keep clear of the wheels of oncoming wagons. He did that by driving on the right side of the road



The British, however, kept to the left. They had smaller wagons, and the driver sat on the wagon, usually on the right side of the front seat. From there he could use his long whip in his right hand without entangling it in the cargo behind him. In that position, on the right side of the wagon, the driver could judge the safety margin of overtaking traffic by keeping to the left side of the road. Countries that became part of the British Empire adopted the keep-left rule too, although there were some exceptions. Canada, for example, where the maritime provinces and Vancouver (later to become British Columbia) drove on the left, eventually changed to the right in order to make border crossings to and from the United States easier. Nova Scotia switched to driving on the right on April 15, 1923. During World War II, American truck makers Ford, Chevrolet and Chrysler built 'Canadian Military Pattern' [CMP] trucks for use throughout the British Empire and most were right hand drive to use in left hand traffic countries



</answer>



<asker's comment>



What an informative answer!



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



Herfnerd
Member since: 11 March 2008
Total points: 7452 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



About a quarter of the world drives on the left and the majority of them are old British colonies.



Its a throwback to olden days when everybody travelled on the left side of the road because that was the most sensible option for feudal, violent societies.  Swordsmen preferred to keep to the left in order to have their right arm nearer to an opponent and their scabbard further from him. Moreover, it reduced the chance of the scabbard (worn on the left) hitting other people.



It was also easier for right handed people to mount/dismount horses on the left - better than on the right and onto the path of oncoming traffic.



</answer>



<answer from>



Technobu...
Member since: 04 December 2007
Total points: 4848 (Level 4)



Contributing In:



Engineering



Maintenance & Repairs



</answer from>



<answer>



"Rhymes with orange" has done his homework.
In Australia we drive on the left, seated on the right side.
For Stephen K and others with a similar opinion,  I would feel very much out of place if I had to change gear with my RIGHT hand. I would be "all at sea"- until I had enough practice.



Same thing.
It's all a matter of how you learned. There is absolutely no problem at all driving on the left for someone who has always done so.
I fly. I sit in the left seat. The left hand is on the control column. That's not a problem either. A light aircraft is controlled in flight solely by the left hand most of the time. The right is for throttle, propeller, flaps, trim, whatever. There is no time when it is necessary to use both hands for the job.
When you need to use both hands for something in the cockpit, you fly NO hands, often. That's illegal in cars.
In the end it boils down to be rather like the "Which came first... the chicken or the egg?" dilemma.
One might also ask of Americans, why do they compulsively change the spelling of English words? It's English language!



</answer>



<answer from>

Member since: 18 July 2007
Total points: 677 (Level 2)

<question>



What is better - 1 hour, 1.5 hour or 2 hour driving lessons?



</question>



<answer from>



Britblok...
Member since: 07 January 2008
Total points: 1704 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Notice a pattern in the answers you're getting?



It's simple - 'everyone' is different - you should do whatever suites you. Don't be swayed by anyone else, including your instructor.



Driving schools will always try to persuade you that 2 hour lessons are best - but that's because that's what suits THEM! Some people find 2 hour lessons very hard because they get tired - some people find them just right.



For lots of free detail about how to decide, click here:



http://www.drivinghelp.com/finding_an_ad...



Personally, I think 1.5 hours is a very good compromise.



Source(s):



http://www.DrivingHelp.com



</answer>



<asker's comment>



Thanks!



</asker's comment>

Member since: 19 June 2008

Hiya. When I had mine years ago I had 2 hour lessons because then it gave my instructor a chance to talk to me and go through things and then for me to drive to further away places and try any parking etc that I needed to do and we drove around the place where I would be taking the test aswell. I did mine in the evenings in October and that was good as I was mostly driving in the dark and am used to it loads, which is good. Some people think 2 hours is to long but from personal experience I think its much more better and you get more out of it. If you have a hour ny the time your in the car and chatted to the instructor and the had a little drive and tried a few things its time to go back.



</answer>

If you get 1 x 2hr lesson, you will be doing nothing but driving in the second hour, no need to get used to the car again, because you are still in it.



Good luck!

One-hour at a time is long enough. Anything longer, and fatigue sets in. Even experienced drivers are urged to stop the vehicle and take a break from time to time. Get out and walk around the car, if nothing else.



</answer>

<question>



I just hit somebodies car and drove off?



i hit somebodies car and dented it. i want to go back but im afraid. it was at a voting place. what do i do?



</question>



<answer from>



Parabola...
Member since: 24 July 2008
Total points: 2272 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Don't worry.  I've done that and I've seen it done.  Just forget about it.



</answer>



<asker's comment>



you're right



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



J-Louis
Member since: 27 October 2008
Total points: 98 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



Morally, you should go back and talk to the driver.



Logistically, if the car was just parked, and you don't think anyone got your license plate #, you should probably do your best to forget about it.



You committed a hit and run, if you go back you will get caught for a hit and run.  Also your insurance will go up.



Rationally speaking, it makes more sense to not go back.



</answer>



<answer from>

<question from>

</question from>



<question>



What kind of auto parts could I add to my car that will help my cars fuel efficiency?



I have an 07' scion tc that sucks on gas mileage.  Will something like a cold air intake or exhaust system help?



</question>



<answer from>



thejeepy...
Member since: 22 September 2008
Total points: 540 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



Both of the items you suggested will help. Also keep the tires inflated correctly and keep your foot out of the gas.



</answer>



<answer from>



cvera91
Member since: 04 June 2007
Total points: 45 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



i don't think there's anything for that. just keep your car well-maintained



</answer>



<answer from>



nonyahbu...
Member since: 25 April 2006
Total points: 2156 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



Typically cold air and exhaust systems help fuel/exhaust flow better which means more power which equals MORE gas used.



1. Make sure car is completely tuned up- plugs, airfilter, etc
2. Eliminate excess weight such as anything you don't have to carry around kept in your car.
3. Tires inflated to max pressure, balanced, wheels aligned.
4. Predict stops ahead by letting off gas, coasting to a stop, and trying NOT to use the brakes.
5. Accellerate slowly, do not punch the gas.



If the car is automatic transmission you will use more gas than a manual...not much can be done.



Source(s):



Search the net some of the "extreme" areodynamics people have done to their cars/trucks.  I don't think you want to install an aero bell pan but it could be done...



</answer>



<answer from>



focus
Member since: 18 May 2007
Total points: 8479 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



Stick a drawing pin on the accelerator pedal with the point upwards and drive with no right shoe.  I'll bet your fuel consumption improves very quickly.



</answer>



<answer from>



FlashDar...
Member since: 23 June 2008
Total points: 1026 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



And don't fall for any of those magic "run your car on water"- or "intake turbulence"- type scams.  None of those things work.



</answer>

<question from>



Mr Cray Zay
Member since: 21 May 2008
Total points: 6902 (Level 5)



</question from>



<question>



What does 'hard ride mean'?



and so what does 'soft ride' mean?



</question>



<answer from>



Dippermo...
Member since: 29 March 2007
Total points: 324 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



A 'hard ride' means fairly stiff suspension, which means you can feel each bump in the road, but you can go round corners quite quickly.  A 'soft ride', not unreasonably, is where you don't feel the bumps in the road but the car tends to wallow in corners.  Pays your money and takes your choice!



</answer>



<answer from>



randall flagg
Member since: 18 June 2007
Total points: 4840 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



hard suspension= more bumpy/ hard Ride
Soft suspension= soft Ride/ smooth Ride



</answer>

<question from>



Grindcor...
Member since: 24 September 2008
Total points: 109 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



How many miles will my Punto do with 10 L of petrol?



well....im driving to oxford academy from ME2 3QF...and the return jouney....i need to know how many L of petrol i need....my Punto is a '95 1.1L engine



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



A 95 punto with a 1.1 engine should spend 6,5l per 100km ( I'm adjusting because the age of the car should make him more thirsty, if it's perfectly maintained remove 0,5 L), that's 153 Km or 95 miles with 10L.



</answer>

<answer>



yokohama, pirelli, goodyear, bf goodrich.  all about the same level, generally one step behind the quality of a michelin tire, which has been rated the #1 replacement tire for eons.  you'll find that when you go look for tires, most of these companies are within a few dollars of each other, including michelin, so it may not help.



in my experience, toyo, hankook and kuzmo are a tier down in both traction and tread life.



</answer>

<answer>

you do have to ask for one, most companies have it as an option now, it costs extra and requires that you have at least a midsized car.
